Taqlid and the Inherent Hesitation in Blind Faith
This passage defines taqlid and argues that the muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what taqlid is and why the muqallid's faith is inherently accompanied by hesitation (tarded), according to the passage.

Core Idea
Blind faith inherently carries hesitation, as certainty without evidence is vulnerable.

This lesson examines the opening argument of the passage: that every muqallid's faith is not free from tarded (hesitation). We will see how the author defines taqlid and why this hesitation invalidates the muqallid's faith on certain definitions of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully to understand the definition of taqlid and the claim that the muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation.
Notice the phrase 'lam yakhl min tarded'.
2
Identify the definition of taqlid
Find where the passage defines taqlid as accepting another's statement without knowing its evidence.
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'tarded'
The passage offers two interpretations: either it refers to scholarly disagreement about the ruling, or to potential future doubt due to lack of evidence.
The preferred interpretation is the second one, as it avoids contradiction with the muqallid's current certainty.
4
Reflect on why hesitation contradicts true faith
Consider why true faith requires certainty free from hesitation, and how the muqallid's lack of evidential grounding makes his faith incomplete.
This links to the definitions of iman discussed later.

The ruling on the faith of the muqallid (blind follower) and an exposition of the disagreement occurring in it. Since everyone who practices taqlid (blind following) in tawhid (monotheism), his faith is not free from hesitation. Then he gave the reason for the obligation of the prior knowledge by his statement: 'Since everyone who' (1), meaning: We only obligated knowledge of what was mentioned (2) by evidence because when he is qualified (3) to understand the proofs, the obligated person is knowing - he is - obligated. (1) This speech - as the commentator said - is a reasoning for the obligation of knowledge which he mentioned in his statement: 'Everyone who is certainly and obligatorily upon him to know... to the end.' So it is as if the author said: It is only obligatory legally upon the obligated person to know what we mentioned, because the one who practices taqlid... to the end. So 'idh' in his speech is a particle indicating reasoning. And taqlid is that you take the statement of another without knowing the evidence of that statement. If you know the evidence upon which the author of the statement you took relied, you are not a muqallid. And what is meant by 'his faith' in his statement: 'his faith is not free from hesitation' is his tasdiq (assent) that follows his jazzm (certainty) concerning the judgments of tawhid without evidence. The best interpretation of hesitation in this expression is that it means the disagreement of scholars about it in the ways that the commentator mentioned and we will mention in detail. And upon this, what comes later in the author's speech is an explanation of this summary. This is better than interpreting hesitation as confusion from the obligated person himself, because its apparent meaning indicates the jazzm that he expressed with 'his faith' and it combines confusion and hesitation, while it is known that when a person is certain of something, he has no hesitation at all. But it is possible to answer this by saying that there is an omitted genitive in the expression, and the estimation is: his faith is not free from acceptance of hesitation, meaning he is certain now, but due to his lack of knowledge of the evidence, he is not safe from hesitation occurring to him. And there is no doubt that what does not require objection and answer is more appropriate to adopt. (2) His statement: 'what was mentioned' meaning from the obligatory, impossible, and permissible regarding Allah and His messengers. (Al-Shanwani). (3) His statement: 'because when he was... etc.' This reasoning is harmful because it implies that the disagreement is only concerning one who has capacity, but it is not so; rather it is absolute. So it is better to omit it because some of the following statements are absolute and some are= even summary and 'qallada' (he practiced taqlid) another: meaning he adopted his statement in the judgments of tawhid, meaning the science of Islamic beliefs, without a proof (1), and without reflection on the creation of the heavens and the earth. (His faith) meaning: his jazzm with what he took from the judgments of tawhid from another without evidence upon it. (Is not free) meaning: it does not escape from hesitation) meaning: hesitation and confusion; rather it is accompanied by it, and that is contrary to faith based on the fact that it is knowledge itself (2) or inner speech, as will come. And the absolute and detailed come only if the subject is the muqallid insofar as he is qualified or not. Al-Shanwani. (1) His statement: (without a proof) excludes students after the shaykhs guide them to evidences, for they are knowers after that. And al-Sanusi gave in his commentary on al-Jazairiyya an example of the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looked for the crescent, and some of them preceded in seeing it; they informed the latecomers and they believed them without sight; they were muqallids. But if the first group guided them by signs until the second also found the crescent and saw it, they became independent of taqlid. Do you not see that the first, when asked about the crescent, would answer: 'They said it appeared.' The second says: 'I saw it with my own eye.' (2) His statement: 'based on it being knowledge itself' is the statement of Imam al-Ash'ari, and upon this faith becomes synonymous with knowledge; if knowledge is negated, faith is negated, because negation of one synonym indicates negation of the other. Al-Amir. And know that the statements concerning faith are three: the commentator mentioned two of them, and the third is inner speech that follows jazzm. For jazzm sometimes is accompanied by tasdiq and sometimes by denial. What is meant by tasdiq is his statement after jazzm as a verbal heart statement: 'I accept that and I believe.' So tasdiq is not jazzm, because tasdiq is a follower and jazzm is followed; faith is a follower and jazzm is the followed accompanied by it as a part. This is what is relied upon. And on the first of the two statements of the commentator: faith is jazzm that corresponds to reality based on evidence. If you say: How can jazzm be judged as faith while it exists among the disbelievers? The answer is that whoever says that faith is knowledge also requires tasdiq. So the two statements agree that both matters are necessary, and the disagreement is only whether tasdiq is faith or a condition, so the disagreement is verbal. So from all this it is concluded that the muqallid is a disbeliever according to the two statements of the commentator. And whoever says that his faith is valid says that the interpretation of faith as inner speech that follows knowledge is not an interpretation of the essence of faith, but rather it is an interpretation of =
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, proof, and analysis of the ruling on the faith of a muqallid (one who blindly follows without evidence). It begins by citing a line from the matn: 'Since everyone who practices taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from hesitation.' The commentary then explains this line as a rationale for the obligation of knowledge through evidence. Taqlid is defined as accepting another's statement without knowing its evidence. The passage then addresses a potential contradiction: how can the muqallid's faith be described as 'jazzm' (certainty) if it is also said to involve 'tarded' (hesitation)? It resolves this by interpreting 'tarded' either as scholarly disagreement about the ruling (a meta-level hesitation) or as a potential for future doubt due to lack of evidence, rather than actual present doubt. The preferred interpretation is that the muqallid is certain now but his faith is vulnerable because he cannot defend it. The passage then moves to a detailed discussion of the three opinions on the definition of iman (faith): (1) jazzm (certainty), (2) tasdiq (heartfelt assent), and (3) hadith al-nafs (inner speech). It explains that jazzm is the basis, while tasdiq is a subsequent mental act. On the first two opinions, the muqallid's faith is invalid (he is a kafir) because it lacks either proper jazzm derived from evidence or proper tasdiq. On the third opinion (inner speech), some scholars consider the muqallid's faith valid because inner speech does not require evidential jazzm. The passage concludes by reconciling the three opinions as verbal, agreeing that both jazzm and tasdiq are necessary, but differing on which constitutes faith itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that blind faith (taqlid) is acceptable in matters of belief.
Correction: This passage clarifies that true faith requires evidential certainty; muqallid's faith is inherently accompanied by hesitation, making it invalid on certain definitions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of taqlid?

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation, which invalidates it on definitions of iman that require certainty free from doubt.

?

?

Three Definitions of Iman and Their Impact on the Muqallid
This passage discusses three definitions of iman and their implications for the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will identify the three opinions on the definition of iman (faith) discussed in the passage and understand how each opinion leads to a specific ruling on the muqallid's faith.

Core Idea
Faith is defined either as jazzm, tasdiq, or inner speech; the muqallid's validity depends on which definition is adopted.

This lesson delves into the three definitions of iman mentioned in the passage: jazzm (certainty), tasdiq (heartsfelt assent), and hadith al-nafs (inner speech). The passage explains how each definition interacts with the muqallid's reliance on taqlid, leading to a conclusion that the muqallid is a disbeliever on the first two opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three opinions
Read the passage that lists the three definitions of iman and the commentary's explanation.
Focus on the relationship between jazzm and tasdiq.
2
Understand each definition
Identify the three definitions: jazzm (certainty), tasdiq (assent as a heart statement), and hadith al-nafs (inner speech).
The passage notes that jazzm is the basis, and tasdiq is a subsequent act.
3
Apply to the muqallid
See how the muqallid's lack of evidential jazzm affects his faith under the first two definitions.
The passage concludes he is a kafir on those opinions.
4
Recognize the verbal disagreement
Note that the third opinion (inner speech) allows for the muqallid's faith to be valid, but the disagreement is verbal.
The passage says the two main opinions agree on the necessity of both jazzm and tasdiq.

حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ في التَّوْحِيدِ إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ ثمَّ علل وجوبَ المعرفة السابقة بقوله : (إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ) (۱) أي: إِنَّما أوجبنا معرفة ما ذُكر (۲) بالدليل لأنَّه متى كانَ مُتأهلا (۳) لفهم البراهين المكلّف معرفة هو - كلف (۱) هذا الكلام - كما قال الشارح - تعليل لوجوب المعرفة الذي ذكره بقوله: «فكل من حتما وجبا عليه أن يعرف ـ إلى آخر» فكأنَّ المصنف قد قال: وإنما وجب شرعاً على المكلف معرفة ما ذكرنا ؛ لأنَّ مَن قلَّد إلى آخر. فـ(إذ) في كلامه: حرف دال على التعليل، والتقليد: أن تأخذ بقول غيرك من غير أن تعرف دليل هذا القول ، فإذا عرفت الدليل الذي استند إليه صاحب القول الذي أخذت به لم تكن مقلدا ، والمراد بإيمانه في قوله: «إيمانه لم يخل من ترديد تصديقه التابع لجزمه بأحكام التوحيد من غير دليل، وأحسنُ ما يحمل عليه (الترديد) في هذه العبارة أن يكون المراد به اختلاف العلماء فيه على الوجوه التي ذكرها الشارح وسنذكرها تفصيلا ، وعلى هذا يكون ما يأتي في كلام المصنّف بعد ذلك تفسيرا لهذا المجمَل ، وهذا خيرٌ مِن أن يحمل (الترديد) على التحير من نفس المكلف ؛ فإن ظاهره يفيد الجزم الذي عبر عنه بقوله: «إيمانه» يجامعُ التَّحير والتَّردُّد، مع أَنَّ المعلوم أن الإنسان متى كان جازماً بالشيء لم يكن عنده تردد أصلا ، وإن أمكن الجواب عن هذا بأنَّ في الكلام مضافًا محذوفًا، والتقدير: إيمانه لم يخل عن قبول ترديد، يعني أنه جازم الآن، ولكنه لعدم معرفته بالدليل لا يؤمن أن يطرأ عليه الترديد، ولا شكّ أنَّ ما لا يُحْوج إلى الاعتراض والجواب أولى أن يؤخذ به. (۲) قوله : ( ما ذكر أي من الواجب والمستحيل والجائز في حق الله وحق رسله. الشنواني . ) : (۳) قوله : (لأنه متى كان ... إلخ) هذا التعليل مضر ؛ لأنه يقتضي أن الخلاف إنما هو فيمن عنده أهلية وليس كذلك، بل هو مطلق، فالأولى حذفه ؛ لأن بعض الأقوال الآتية يطلق وبعضها= ولو إجمالية و(قَلَّدَ) غيره : أي أخذ بقوله (في) أحكام (التَّوْحِيدِ) يعني عِلْمٍ العقائدِ الإسلاميَّةِ مِن غير حُجَّةٍ (۱) ، ولا تفكر في خَلْق السَّماوات والأرض (إِيمَانُهُ ) أي : جَزْمُه بما أخذَه من أحكام التَّوحيد من غيره بلا دليل عليه (لَمْ يَخْلُ) أي: لا يَسْلَمُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ) أي: تردُّدٍ وتحير، بل هو مصحوب به، وذلكَ يُنافي الإيمان بناءً على أنَّه : نفسُ المعرفة (٢) ، أو حديث النَّفس التَّابعُ يفصل كما يأتي ، ولا يأتي الإطلاق والتفصيل إلا إذا كان الموضوع: المقلد من حيث هو متأهلا أو لا . الشنواني. (۱) قوله: (من غير حجة خرجت التلامذة بعدَ أنْ يُرشدهم الأشياخ للأدلة ، فهم عارفون بعد، وضرب السنوسي في شرح الجزائرية مثالا للفرق بينهم وبين المقلدين بجماعة نظروا للهلال فسبق بعضهم لرؤيته ؛ فأخبروا الجامعة المسبوقة وصدقوهم من غير معاينة كانوا مقلدين ، وإن أرشدتهم - الجماعةُ الأولى التي سبقت ـ بالعلامات حتى عثرت الثانية أيضا على الهلال وعاينوه استقلوا عن التقليد، ألا ترى أنَّ الأولى إذا سئلت عن الهلال كان جوابها: قالوا إنه ظهر . والثانية تقول: إني رأيته بعيني (۲) قوله: (بناء على أنه نفس المعرفة هو قول الإمام الأشعري وعليه فيكون الإيمان مرادفا انتفت المعرفة انتفى الإيمان ؛ لأن انتفاء أحد المترادفين يدل على انتفاء الآخر. الأمير. واعلم أن الأقوال في الإيمان ثلاثة ذكر الشارح منها قولين، والثالث حديث النفس التابع للجزم، فالجزم تارة يجامعه تصديق وتارة يجامعه إنكار ، والمراد بالتصديق قوله بعد الجزم قولاً قلبيًا : قبلتُ ذلك وآمنت، فالتصديق غير الجزم ؛ لأن التصديق تابع والجزم متبوع، والإيمان هو تابع والجزم هو المتبوع مصاحب به جزء، وهذا هو المعتمد وعلى القول الأول من قولي الشارح: الإيمان هو الجزم المطابق للواقع عن دليل. فإن قلت: كيف يكون الحكم على الجزم بأنه الإيمان مع وجوده من الكفار ؟ فالجواب أن من يقول بأن الإيمان هو المعرفة يشترط التصديق، فاتفق القولان على أنه لا بد من مجموع الأمرين، والخلاف إنما هو في كون التصديق هو الإيمان أو شرط ، فالخلف لفظي ، فتحصل من هذا كله أن المقلد كافر على قولي الشارح ، ومن قال بصحة إيمانه قال: إن تفسير الإيمان بحديث النفس التابع للمعرفة ليس تفسيرا لأصل الإيمان ، وإنما هو تفسير =
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid (blind follower) and an exposition of the disagreement occurring in it. Since everyone who practices taqlid (blind following) in tawhid (monotheism), his faith is not free from hesitation. Then he gave the reason for the obligation of the prior knowledge by his statement: 'Since everyone who' (1), meaning: We only obligated knowledge of what was mentioned (2) by evidence because when he is qualified (3) to understand the proofs, the obligated person is knowing - he is - obligated. (1) This speech - as the commentator said - is a reasoning for the obligation of knowledge which he mentioned in his statement: 'Everyone who is certainly and obligatorily upon him to know... to the end.' So it is as if the author said: It is only obligatory legally upon the obligated person to know what we mentioned, because the one who practices taqlid... to the end. So 'idh' in his speech is a particle indicating reasoning. And taqlid is that you take the statement of another without knowing the evidence of that statement. If you know the evidence upon which the author of the statement you took relied, you are not a muqallid. And what is meant by 'his faith' in his statement: 'his faith is not free from hesitation' is his tasdiq (assent) that follows his jazzm (certainty) concerning the judgments of tawhid without evidence. The best interpretation of hesitation in this expression is that it means the disagreement of scholars about it in the ways that the commentator mentioned and we will mention in detail. And upon this, what comes later in the author's speech is an explanation of this summary. This is better than interpreting hesitation as confusion from the obligated person himself, because its apparent meaning indicates the jazzm that he expressed with 'his faith' and it combines confusion and hesitation, while it is known that when a person is certain of something, he has no hesitation at all. But it is possible to answer this by saying that there is an omitted genitive in the expression, and the estimation is: his faith is not free from acceptance of hesitation, meaning he is certain now, but due to his lack of knowledge of the evidence, he is not safe from hesitation occurring to him. And there is no doubt that what does not require objection and answer is more appropriate to adopt. (2) His statement: 'what was mentioned' meaning from the obligatory, impossible, and permissible regarding Allah and His messengers. (Al-Shanwani). (3) His statement: 'because when he was... etc.' This reasoning is harmful because it implies that the disagreement is only concerning one who has capacity, but it is not so; rather it is absolute. So it is better to omit it because some of the following statements are absolute and some are= even summary and 'qallada' (he practiced taqlid) another: meaning he adopted his statement in the judgments of tawhid, meaning the science of Islamic beliefs, without a proof (1), and without reflection on the creation of the heavens and the earth. (His faith) meaning: his jazzm with what he took from the judgments of tawhid from another without evidence upon it. (Is not free) meaning: it does not escape from hesitation) meaning: hesitation and confusion; rather it is accompanied by it, and that is contrary to faith based on the fact that it is knowledge itself (2) or inner speech, as will come. And the absolute and detailed come only if the subject is the muqallid insofar as he is qualified or not. Al-Shanwani. (1) His statement: (without a proof) excludes students after the shaykhs guide them to evidences, for they are knowers after that. And al-Sanusi gave in his commentary on al-Jazairiyya an example of the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looked for the crescent, and some of them preceded in seeing it; they informed the latecomers and they believed them without sight; they were muqallids. But if the first group guided them by signs until the second also found the crescent and saw it, they became independent of taqlid. Do you not see that the first, when asked about the crescent, would answer: 'They said it appeared.' The second says: 'I saw it with my own eye.' (2) His statement: 'based on it being knowledge itself' is the statement of Imam al-Ash'ari, and upon this faith becomes synonymous with knowledge; if knowledge is negated, faith is negated, because negation of one synonym indicates negation of the other. Al-Amir. And know that the statements concerning faith are three: the commentator mentioned two of them, and the third is inner speech that follows jazzm. For jazzm sometimes is accompanied by tasdiq and sometimes by denial. What is meant by tasdiq is his statement after jazzm as a verbal heart statement: 'I accept that and I believe.' So tasdiq is not jazzm, because tasdiq is a follower and jazzm is followed; faith is a follower and jazzm is the followed accompanied by it as a part. This is what is relied upon. And on the first of the two statements of the commentator: faith is jazzm that corresponds to reality based on evidence. If you say: How can jazzm be judged as faith while it exists among the disbelievers? The answer is that whoever says that faith is knowledge also requires tasdiq. So the two statements agree that both matters are necessary, and the disagreement is only whether tasdiq is faith or a condition, so the disagreement is verbal. So from all this it is concluded that the muqallid is a disbeliever according to the two statements of the commentator. And whoever says that his faith is valid says that the interpretation of faith as inner speech that follows knowledge is not an interpretation of the essence of faith, but rather it is an interpretation of =
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, proof, and analysis of the ruling on the faith of a muqallid (one who blindly follows without evidence). It begins by citing a line from the matn: 'Since everyone who practices taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from hesitation.' The commentary then explains this line as a rationale for the obligation of knowledge through evidence. Taqlid is defined as accepting another's statement without knowing its evidence. The passage then addresses a potential contradiction: how can the muqallid's faith be described as 'jazzm' (certainty) if it is also said to involve 'tarded' (hesitation)? It resolves this by interpreting 'tarded' either as scholarly disagreement about the ruling (a meta-level hesitation) or as a potential for future doubt due to lack of evidence, rather than actual present doubt. The preferred interpretation is that the muqallid is certain now but his faith is vulnerable because he cannot defend it. The passage then moves to a detailed discussion of the three opinions on the definition of iman (faith): (1) jazzm (certainty), (2) tasdiq (heartfelt assent), and (3) hadith al-nafs (inner speech). It explains that jazzm is the basis, while tasdiq is a subsequent mental act. On the first two opinions, the muqallid's faith is invalid (he is a kafir) because it lacks either proper jazzm derived from evidence or proper tasdiq. On the third opinion (inner speech), some scholars consider the muqallid's faith valid because inner speech does not require evidential jazzm. The passage concludes by reconciling the three opinions as verbal, agreeing that both jazzm and tasdiq are necessary, but differing on which constitutes faith itself.

Key Terms
إيمان
جزم
تصديق
حديث النفس

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which definition(s) of iman is the muqallid considered a disbeliever?
A) On all three definitions
B) Only on the definition of inner speech
C) On the first two definitions (jazzm and tasdiq)
D) On none of the definitions

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is invalid on the first two definitions of iman (jazzm and tasdiq) but may be considered valid on the definition of inner speech, though the disagreement is verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on blind imitation in your faith without seeking personal certainty? How do you know your iman is based on conviction and not just following others?
?
The passage describes that the muqallid's jazzm is not derived from evidence. Consider your own beliefs: do you have evidence for the fundamentals of faith, or do you rely on what others say? What is one step you can take today to move from imitation to certainty?
?
If your iman were tested tomorrow, would it stand on the strength of your own conviction or crumble because it never truly became your own? What will you do to ensure your faith is rooted in knowledge and certainty?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
Reconciling the Opinions: The Muqallid's Faith Between Validity and Invalidity
This passage reconciles the three opinions and concludes that the muqallid is a disbeliever on the two main opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the passage reconciles the three opinions on iman and why the disagreement about the muqallid is ultimately verbal, focusing on the essential requirement of both jazzm and tasdiq.

Core Idea
All opinions agree that both jazzm and tasdiq are needed; the verbal dispute is over which one constitutes iman.

This lesson synthesizes the previous discussions, showing how the passage resolves apparent contradictions among the definitions of iman. It highlights that the two main opinions (jazzm with evidence, and knowledge with tasdiq) both require the same elements, differing only on terminology. This verbal nature means the muqallid's fate depends on which definition one adopts, but the core requirement of certainty based on evidence remains.

Guided Path
1
Review the two main opinions
Read the passage's conclusion where it states that the muqallid is a kafir on the two opinions, and then explains the verbal disagreement.
2
Understand the reconciliation
The passage says that both opinions agree on the need for both jazzm and tasdiq; they only differ on whether tasdiq is the essence of faith or a condition.
This makes the disagreement verbal, not substantive.
3
Implication for the muqallid
On either opinion, the muqallid lacks evidential jazzm, so he fails to meet the requirement. The third opinion (inner speech) offers a possible exception.
The passage notes that the third opinion is not about the essence of faith.
4
Final takeaway
Reflect on how this analysis shows that true faith requires evidential certainty, and blind following is insufficient.

Then he gave the reason for the obligation of the prior knowledge by his statement: 'Since everyone who' (1), meaning: We only obligated knowledge of what was mentioned (2) by evidence because when he is qualified (3) to understand the proofs, the obligated person is knowing - he is - obligated. (1) This speech - as the commentator said - is a reasoning for the obligation of knowledge which he mentioned in his statement: 'Everyone who is certainly and obligatorily upon him to know... to the end.' So it is as if the author said: It is only obligatory legally upon the obligated person to know what we mentioned, because the one who practices taqlid... to the end. So 'idh' in his speech is a particle indicating reasoning. And taqlid is that you take the statement of another without knowing the evidence of that statement. If you know the evidence upon which the author of the statement you took relied, you are not a muqallid. And what is meant by 'his faith' in his statement: 'his faith is not free from hesitation' is his tasdiq (assent) that follows his jazzm (certainty) concerning the judgments of tawhid without evidence. The best interpretation of hesitation in this expression is that it means the disagreement of scholars about it in the ways that the commentator mentioned and we will mention in detail. And upon this, what comes later in the author's speech is an explanation of this summary. This is better than interpreting hesitation as confusion from the obligated person himself, because its apparent meaning indicates the jazzm that he expressed with 'his faith' and it combines confusion and hesitation, while it is known that when a person is certain of something, he has no hesitation at all. But it is possible to answer this by saying that there is an omitted genitive in the expression, and the estimation is: his faith is not free from acceptance of hesitation, meaning he is certain now, but due to his lack of knowledge of the evidence, he is not safe from hesitation occurring to him. And there is no doubt that what does not require objection and answer is more appropriate to adopt. (2) His statement: 'what was mentioned' meaning from the obligatory, impossible, and permissible regarding Allah and His messengers. (Al-Shanwani). (3) His statement: 'because when he was... etc.' This reasoning is harmful because it implies that the disagreement is only concerning one who has capacity, but it is not so; rather it is absolute. So it is better to omit it because some of the following statements are absolute and some are= even summary and 'qallada' (he practiced taqlid) another: meaning he adopted his statement in the judgments of tawhid, meaning the science of Islamic beliefs, without a proof (1), and without reflection on the creation of the heavens and the earth. (His faith) meaning: his jazzm with what he took from the judgments of tawhid from another without evidence upon it. (Is not free) meaning: it does not escape from hesitation) meaning: hesitation and confusion; rather it is accompanied by it, and that is contrary to faith based on the fact that it is knowledge itself (2) or inner speech, as will come. And the absolute and detailed come only if the subject is the muqallid insofar as he is qualified or not. Al-Shanwani. (1) His statement: (without a proof) excludes students after the shaykhs guide them to evidences, for they are knowers after that. And al-Sanusi gave in his commentary on al-Jazairiyya an example of the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looked for the crescent, and some of them preceded in seeing it; they informed the latecomers and they believed them without sight; they were muqallids. But if the first group guided them by signs until the second also found the crescent and saw it, they became independent of taqlid. Do you not see that the first, when asked about the crescent, would answer: 'They said it appeared.' The second says: 'I saw it with my own eye.' (2) His statement: 'based on it being knowledge itself' is the statement of Imam al-Ash'ari, and upon this faith becomes synonymous with knowledge; if knowledge is negated, faith is negated, because negation of one synonym indicates negation of the other. Al-Amir. And know that the statements concerning faith are three: the commentator mentioned two of them, and the third is inner speech that follows jazzm. For jazzm sometimes is accompanied by tasdiq and sometimes by denial. What is meant by tasdiq is his statement after jazzm as a verbal heart statement: 'I accept that and I believe.' So tasdiq is not jazzm, because tasdiq is a follower and jazzm is followed; faith is a follower and jazzm is the followed accompanied by it as a part. This is what is relied upon. And on the first of the two statements of the commentator: faith is jazzm that corresponds to reality based on evidence. If you say: How can jazzm be judged as faith while it exists among the disbelievers? The answer is that whoever says that faith is knowledge also requires tasdiq. So the two statements agree that both matters are necessary, and the disagreement is only whether tasdiq is faith or a condition, so the disagreement is verbal. So from all this it is concluded that the muqallid is a disbeliever according to the two statements of the commentator. And whoever says that his faith is valid says that the interpretation of faith as inner speech that follows knowledge is not an interpretation of the essence of faith, but rather it is an interpretation of =
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, proof, and analysis of the ruling on the faith of a muqallid (one who blindly follows without evidence). It begins by citing a line from the matn: 'Since everyone who practices taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from hesitation.' The commentary then explains this line as a rationale for the obligation of knowledge through evidence. Taqlid is defined as accepting another's statement without knowing its evidence. The passage then addresses a potential contradiction: how can the muqallid's faith be described as 'jazzm' (certainty) if it is also said to involve 'tarded' (hesitation)? It resolves this by interpreting 'tarded' either as scholarly disagreement about the ruling (a meta-level hesitation) or as a potential for future doubt due to lack of evidence, rather than actual present doubt. The preferred interpretation is that the muqallid is certain now but his faith is vulnerable because he cannot defend it. The passage then moves to a detailed discussion of the three opinions on the definition of iman (faith): (1) jazzm (certainty), (2) tasdiq (heartfelt assent), and (3) hadith al-nafs (inner speech). It explains that jazzm is the basis, while tasdiq is a subsequent mental act. On the first two opinions, the muqallid's faith is invalid (he is a kafir) because it lacks either proper jazzm derived from evidence or proper tasdiq. On the third opinion (inner speech), some scholars consider the muqallid's faith valid because inner speech does not require evidential jazzm. The passage concludes by reconciling the three opinions as verbal, agreeing that both jazzm and tasdiq are necessary, but differing on which constitutes faith itself.

خلاف لفظي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the three definitions of iman are mutually exclusive and lead to contradictory rulings.
Correction: The passage shows the first two opinions actually agree on the essential components, and the disagreement is verbal.

Checkpoint

D) The difference is only about the muqallid's faith

Key Takeaway
The muqallid is a disbeliever on the two main opinions, which agree on the necessity of evidential jazzm, making the disagreement about the definition of iman verbal.

The Three Opinions on Iman and the Role of Tasdiq
Shanwani enumerates three opinions on iman and defines the relationship between conviction and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on the definition of iman and the distinction between conviction (jzm) and heart-acceptance (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Iman is conviction accompanied by tasdiq, not conviction alone.

Shanwani begins by enumerating the three views on iman held by scholars. He highlights the relied-upon view that distinguishes between conviction (jzm) and the heart's verbal acceptance (tasdiq). This distinction is essential for later discussions about the muqallid. Let's examine the definitions carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the passage and list the three opinions: the two mentioned by al-Sanusi and the third (inner speech).
The third opinion is hadith al-nafs (inner speech).
2
Understand the definition of tasdiq
Shanwani defines tasdiq as a heart-statement after conviction. Distinguish it from mere conviction.
3
Grasp the relied-upon relationship
Note that iman is described as a follower of conviction, with conviction being the followed and accompanied by tasdiq as a part.
This is the relied-upon view.
4
Reflect on its significance
Think about why this matters for the later ruling on the muqallid. The muqallid has conviction but not tasdiq or proper evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الأقوال في الإيمان ثلاثة ذكر الشارح منها قولين، والثالث حديث النفس التابع للجزم، فالجزم تارة يجامعه تصديق وتارة يجامعه إنكار، والمراد بالتصديق قوله بعد الجزم قولاً قلبيًا: قبلتُ ذلك وآمنت، فالتصديق غير الجزم؛ لأن التصديق تابع والجزم متبوع، والإيمان هو تابع والجزم هو المتبوع مصاحب به جزء، وهذا هو المعتمد، وعلى القول الأول من قولي الشارح: الإيمان هو الجزم المطابق للواقع عن دليل. فإن قلت: كيف يكون الحكم على الجزم بأنه الإيمان مع وجوده من الكفار؟ فالجواب أن من يقول بأن الإيمان هو المعرفة يشترط التصديق، فاتفق القولان على أنه لا بد من مجموع الأمرين، والخلاف إنما هو في كون التصديق هو الإيمان أو شرط، فالخلف لفظي، فتحصل من هذا كله أن المقلد كافر على قولي الشارح، ومن قال بصحة إيمانه قال: إن تفسير الإيمان بحديث النفس التابع للمعرفة ليس تفسيرا لأصل الإيمان، وإنما هو تفسير للمعرفة. وأما أصل الإيمان فهو حديث النفس التابع للجزم ولو لم يكن عن دليل، فتحصل من هذا أن الإيمان بسيط. الشنواني.
Know that the opinions concerning iman are three: the commentator mentioned two of them, and the third is the inner speech that follows conviction. For conviction is sometimes accompanied by tasdiq and sometimes by denial. By tasdiq is meant one's saying after conviction, as a heart-statement: 'I accept that and believe.' Thus tasdiq is other than conviction, because tasdiq is the follower and conviction is the followed. Iman is the follower and conviction is the followed, accompanied by it as a part. And this is the relied-upon view. According to the first of the commentator's two opinions, iman is conviction that corresponds to reality based on evidence. If you say: How can conviction be judged as iman when it exists among disbelievers? The answer is that one who says iman is knowledge requires tasdiq. So both opinions agree that both matters are necessary, and the disagreement is only whether tasdiq is the essence of iman or a condition; so the dispute is verbal. Thus from all this it follows that the muqallid is a disbeliever according to the commentator's two opinions. And one who says his faith is valid says that the interpretation of iman as inner speech that follows knowledge is not an interpretation of the essence of iman, but rather an interpretation of knowledge. As for the essence of iman, it is inner speech that follows conviction, even if not from evidence. From this it follows that iman is simple. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument about the definition of iman and its implication for the muqallid. It begins by stating that there are three opinions on iman: the commentator (al-Sanusi) mentioned two, and the third is that iman is inner speech (hadith al-nafs) that follows conviction (jzm). It then explains that conviction can be accompanied by either tasdiq (heart-acceptance) or denial. Tasdiq is defined as the heart's verbal affirmation after conviction: 'I accept and believe.' Thus tasdiq is distinct from conviction: tasdiq is a follower, conviction is the followed. Iman is the follower, and conviction is the followed accompanied by it as a part. This is the relied-upon view. According to the first of the commentator's two opinions, iman is conviction that matches reality and is based on evidence. An objection is raised: disbelievers also have conviction about their disbelief, so why is that not iman? The answer: those who define iman as knowledge require tasdiq. Thus both opinions agree that both conviction and tasdiq are needed; the difference is only whether tasdiq is the essence or a condition—a verbal dispute. Therefore, based on the commentator's two opinions, the muqallid (whose conviction lacks evidence) is a disbeliever. The third opinion, which says his faith is valid, argues that interpreting iman as inner speech that follows knowledge is not an interpretation of the essence of iman but of knowledge. The essence of iman is inner speech that follows conviction, even without evidence. Thus iman is simple (not composite).

Key Terms
إيمان
جزم
تصديق
حديث النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, what is the relationship between iman and conviction?
A) Iman is identical to conviction
B) Iman is the follower of conviction and conviction is the followed, accompanied by it as a part
C) Iman is independent of conviction
D) Iman is the same as inner speech

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon view distinguishes iman from mere conviction by requiring tasdiq as a part of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, do you merely acknowledge its truth (conviction) or do you actively utter in your heart, 'I accept and believe'? Which of these two actions is more present in your daily life?
?
Think of a moment when you were convinced of a truth about Allah but resisted submitting to it. What kept you from tasdiq? Will you confess that resistance to Allah today and ask Him to grant you heart-acceptance?
?
The passage says tasdiq is a follower of conviction. Examine your salaah: is it merely a routine of belief or are there moments when your heart says, 'I accept and believe'? What one change can you make to ensure tasdiq is alive in your next prayer?

The Ruling on the Muqallid: Disbeliever or Believer?
Shanwani presents an objection and answer, then draws the conclusion about the muqallid and introduces the third opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the muqallid is considered a disbeliever on two of the three opinions on iman, and how the third opinion allows for his faith.

Core Idea
The muqallid's iman depends on whether conviction must be based on evidence.

Having established the definitions of iman, Shanwani now addresses a crucial case: the muqallid—a person who holds conviction without personal evidence. He shows that on Sanusi's two opinions, such a person is considered a disbeliever, but a third opinion holds that his iman is valid because iman is simply inner speech following conviction.

Guided Path
1
Review the first opinion from Sanusi
The first opinion defines iman as conviction based on evidence. This requires that the conviction be 'muṭābiq li-l-wāqiʿ ʿan dalīl.'
2
Understand the objection and answer
The objection is that disbelievers have conviction too. The answer is that those who say iman is knowledge require tasdiq, making it a verbal dispute.
3
Apply to the muqallid
On Sanusi's two opinions, the muqallid lacks evidence for his conviction, so he is a disbeliever.
4
Consider the third opinion
The third opinion redefines iman as inner speech following conviction, even without evidence. Thus the muqallid's iman is valid, and iman is simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that on the first two opinions, a muqallid—someone who believes without personally verifying the evidence—is considered a disbeliever. Examine your own heart: Are you a muqallid in your faith? Do you hold your beliefs about Allah and His Messenger because you have personally investigated the proofs, or because you simply inherited them from your family and community? If you were asked right now to give one solid piece of evidence for your belief in God, could you do it? If not, will you commit today to seeking that evidence, not to feel knowledgeable, but to ensure your iman is truly yours and accepted by Allah?
?
The passage shows that on the third opinion, conviction alone (even without evidence) makes one a believer. That might feel like a safety net. But ask yourself: Are you content with the minimum requirement for faith, or do you want the faith that the Prophet's Companions had—a faith built on clear signs and certainty? Which path will you choose: the easy path of blind acceptance, or the demanding path of seeking knowledge and certainty? Your choice defines not just your iman, but your entire relationship with your Creator.
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment, and He asks you: 'Did you believe in Me because you were convinced by clear evidence, or because you followed others without thinking?' How would you answer? This lesson forces you to confront that question now, while you can still change. Will you continue to put off the effort of learning the proofs, or will you take the first step today—perhaps by studying a book on the evidences of faith—so that your conviction is not just inherited, but earned?

The First Three Opinions on the Muqallid's Faith
Shanwani enumerates the six opinions of scholars on whether the muqallid's faith is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three scholarly opinions on the validity of the muqallid's faith and the basis for their divergence.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree on whether blind imitation in faith is sufficient or sinful.

In this passage, Shanwani summarizes the scholarly debate over the faith of the muqallid—one who accepts beliefs without independent reasoning. He reports six opinions, and here we examine the first three, ranging from outright rejection to conditional acceptance. This lesson will clarify each opinion and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Shanwani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage enumerates three opinions. For each, note the ruling on the muqallid and any conditions mentioned. Pay special attention to the third opinion which distinguishes based on capacity for independent reasoning.
The first opinion deems the muqallid a disbeliever; the second calls him a sinner; the third differentiates.
3
Reflect on Al-Sanusi's retraction
The passage notes that Al-Sanusi initially held the first opinion but later retracted it. Ask yourself why he might have changed his view and what that tells us about scholarly development.
This shows that even great scholars revisit and revise their positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الخلاف في هذه المسألة أنَّ للعلماء فيها ستة أقوال: القول الأول : عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد ، بمعنى عدم صحة تقليد المقلد، فيكون المقلد كافراً ، وجرى على هذا السنوسي في كُبراه، وقد عرفت مما قدمناه أنَّه رجع عن هذا القول . القول الثاني: الاكتفاء بالتقليد مع العصيان مطلقا، أي: سواء وجدت في المقلد أهلية النظر أم لم توجد . القول الثالث: الاكتفاء بالتقليد مع التفصيل، فإن كان عند المقلد أهلية النظر والاستدلال فهو مؤمن عاص؛ لأنه ترك ما يقدر عليه ، وإن لم تكن فيه أهليَّة النَّظر والاستدلال فهو مؤمن.
And the summary of the disagreement in this issue is that the scholars have six opinions on it: The first opinion: not accepting taqlid, meaning the invalidity of the muqallid's taqlid, so the muqallid is a disbeliever. Al-Sanusi followed this in his Major [work], and you know from what we have presented that he retracted this opinion. The second opinion: accepting taqlid with sin absolutely, i.e., whether the muqallid has the capacity for independent reasoning or not. The third opinion: accepting taqlid with detail: if the muqallid has the capacity for independent reasoning and inference, then he is a believing sinner because he neglected what he is capable of; if he does not have the capacity for independent reasoning and inference, then he is a believer.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification. It summarizes the scholarly dispute over the muqallid's faith into six opinions, of which three are detailed here. The first opinion rejects taqlid entirely, deeming the muqallid a disbeliever. Al-Sanusi initially held this view but later retracted. The second opinion accepts taqlid but considers it a sin in all cases, regardless of the muqallid's capability. The third opinion distinguishes based on capacity: if the muqallid is capable of independent reasoning but does not use it, he is a believing sinner; if he is not capable, he is a believer. The passage sets the stage for the remaining three opinions which are presumably given later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is only one valid position on the muqallid's faith.
Correction: The passage shows that scholars have multiple valid opinions, reflecting the complexity of the issue.

B) He is a believer without sin
C) He is a believing sinner

Key Takeaway
The first three opinions show a spectrum from complete rejection of taqlid to nuanced acceptance based on the muqallid's capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you have the capacity to learn and reflect, yet you do not, what does that make you according to the third opinion? Will you take a step today to study even one evidence for God’s existence?
?
The first opinion warns that blind imitation (taqlid) can be so dangerous that one may be considered a disbeliever. If you were to die tonight as a muqallid, would you be content with your state? What will you change starting this week?
?
According to the second opinion, taqlid is always a sin—even if you are incapable of reasoning. Many Muslims rely on scholars without any personal effort. Does this apply to you? Are you satisfied with being sinful in the most fundamental matter of your existence? What is one concrete action you will take to move from blind imitation to informed faith?

The Six Opinions on the Faith of the Muqallid
Enumeration of six scholarly opinions on the faith of the muqallid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six scholarly opinions on the faith of the muqallid and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
There are six valid scholarly opinions on the faith of the muqallid, ranging from outright disbelief to sinlessness.

This passage presents the core disagreement among scholars about the faith status of one who follows religious beliefs without personal reasoning (taqlid). The author lists six distinct opinions, each with its own rationale, and indicates his preferred view. Understanding these positions clarifies an important debate in Islamic theology about the role of reasoning in faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to get an overview of the six opinions. Notice how each opinion is introduced with 'القول'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the first opinion
Focus on the first opinion: taqlid is invalid and the muqallid is a disbeliever. Note that al-Sanusi initially held this but later retracted.
This is the strictest position.
3
Compare second and third opinions
Both opinions consider taqlid sufficient with disobedience, but the third differentiates based on the capacity for reasoning. Grasp the distinction.
The author favors the third opinion.
4
Examine the fourth opinion
This opinion restricts valid taqlid to following sources that yield certainty (Qur'an and mutawatir sunna). Understand the condition.
5
Analyze the fifth and sixth opinions
The fifth opinion treats reasoning as a perfection, not a condition. The sixth forbids reasoning for those at risk. Notice the nuance in the sixth.
These are more lenient positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الخلاف في هذه المسألة أن للعلماء فيها ستة أقوال: القول الأول: عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد، بمعنى عدم صحة تقليد المقلد، فيكون المقلد كافراً، وجرى على هذا السنوسي في كبراه، وقد عرفت مما قدمناه أنه رجع عن هذا القول. القول الثاني: الاكتفاء بالتقليد مع العصيان مطلقا، أي: سواء وجدت في المقلد أهلية النظر أم لم توجد. القول الثالث: الاكتفاء بالتقليد مع التفصيل، فإن كان عند المقلد أهلية النظر والاستدلال فهو مؤمن عاص؛ لأنه ترك ما يقدر عليه، وإن لم تكن فيه أهلية النظر والاستدلال فهو مؤمن غير عاص؛ لأنه ما ترك شيئاً إلا وهو عاجز عن تحصيله، ولا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها، وهذا هو القول الذي رجحناه في كلامنا السابق، وهو الذي تنصره أدلة الشرع. القول الرابع: أنه إذا قلد القرآن والسنة المتواترة التي تفيد القطع؛ كان إيمانه صحيحا لاتباعه المقطوع به، وإن قلد غير ذلك لم يصح إيمانه؛ لأنه لا يأمن الخطأ. القول الخامس: الاكتفاء بالتقليد، وعدم الحكم بعصيانه مطلقا، سواء أكانت عنده أهلية النظر والاستدلال أم لم تكن. وصاحب هذا القول اعتبر النظر والاستدلال شرطاً لكمال الإيمان، لا لأصل الإيمان، فمن لم تكن عنده أهلية النظر فهو غير قادر على تحصيله، ومن كان عنده أهلية النظر، فإن نظر واستدل فقد كمل إيمانه، وإن لم ينظر، ولم يستدل فقد ترك ما الأولى بمن على مثل حاله. القول السادس: أن إيمان المقلد صحيح، وأنه يحرم عليه النظر والاستدلال. ولا شك أن أصحاب هذا القول يريدون تحريم النظر والاستدلال على طريق الفلسفة التي لا تؤمن عاقبتها، وأن يكون قولهم هذا خاصا بمن يخشى عليه من الأخذ في هذه السبيل أن تتزلزل عقيدته بما يطرأ عليها من آراء الفلاسفة ونظرياتهم، فإن كان بحيث لا يخشى عليه شيء من ذلك .. فاعتقادي أنه لا يحرم عليه النظر.
The outcome of the disagreement in this issue is that the scholars have six opinions concerning it: The first opinion: not sufficing with taqlid (following without proof), meaning the invalidity of the taqlid of the muqallid (the imitator), so the muqallid is a disbeliever. Al-Sanusi held this in his Major [Work], and you have known from what we presented that he retracted from this opinion. The second opinion: sufficing with taqlid along with disobedience absolutely, i.e., whether capacity for reasoned inquiry (nazar) is present in the muqallid or not. The third opinion: sufficing with taqlid with differentiation: if the muqallid has the capacity for reasoned inquiry and inference (istidlal), then he is a believer who is disobedient because he left what he was capable of; if he does not have the capacity for reasoned inquiry and inference, then he is a believer not disobedient because he left nothing except that he is incapable of attaining it, and God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. This is the opinion we have preferred in our previous discourse, and it is the one which the proofs of the Law support. The fourth opinion: that if he follows (qallada) the Qur'an and the frequently transmitted sunna which yield certainty, then his faith is valid because of his following what is certain; if he follows other than that, his faith is not valid because he is not safe from error. The fifth opinion: sufficing with taqlid and not judging him disobedient absolutely, whether he has the capacity for reasoned inquiry and inference or not. The holder of this opinion considered reasoned inquiry and inference a condition for the perfection of faith, not for the essence of faith. So one who does not have the capacity for reasoned inquiry is unable to attain it; and one who has the capacity for reasoned inquiry: if he engages in inquiry and inference, he has perfected his faith; if he does not engage in inquiry and inference, he has left what is more appropriate for someone in his situation. The sixth opinion: that the faith of the muqallid is valid, and that reasoned inquiry and inference are forbidden for him. There is no doubt that the holders of this opinion intend the prohibition of reasoned inquiry and inference in the manner of philosophy, whose outcome is not safe, and that this statement of theirs is specific to one who is feared for that if he embarks upon this path, his creed may become shaken by what occurs to him from the opinions and theories of the philosophers. If he is such that nothing of that is feared for him... then my belief is that it is not forbidden for him to engage in reasoned inquiry.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six scholarly opinions on the faith of the muqallid (one who follows religious beliefs without personal reasoning or proof). The author lists each opinion with a brief explanation. The first opinion states that taqlid is insufficient, making the muqallid a kafir; al-Sanusi initially held this but later retracted. The second opinion holds taqlid sufficient but sinful, regardless of the person's capacity for reasoning. The third opinion differentiates based on capacity: if the muqallid is capable of reasoning, he is sinful for not using it; if incapable, he is not sinful. The author favors this third opinion. The fourth opinion restricts valid taqlid to following sources that yield certainty (Qur'an and mutawatir sunna). The fifth opinion considers reasoning a condition for perfection of faith, not its essence, so taqlid is sufficient but the capable person misses out on perfection. The sixth opinion declares taqlid valid and reasoning forbidden for those at risk of harm to their creed, but not absolutely forbidden for those immune to such harm.

تقليد

أهلية
إيمان
كفر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only valid position is that taqlid is completely invalid and the muqallid is a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage shows a spectrum of valid scholarly opinions, with most considering the muqallid a believer, though possibly disobedient.

Checkpoint
According to the third opinion, which the author favors, what is the ruling on the faith of a muqallid who lacks the capacity for reasoned inquiry (nazar)?
A) He is a disbeliever (kafir).
B) He is a believer and not disobedient.
C) He is a believer but disobedient.
D) His faith is invalid because he did not follow a certain source.

Key Takeaway
The faith of a muqallid is a matter of scholarly debate, with six distinct opinions ranging from outright disbelief to full acceptance without sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own beliefs, are you relying on taqlid from scholars you trust? Does this lesson's spectrum of opinions make you question whether your faith is truly your own? What would it take for you to personally engage in nazar (reasoned inquiry) about the foundations of your faith — and if you are capable but haven't, are you disobeying Allah? Commit to one concrete step to move from blind imitation to informed conviction this week.
?
If you are a student of knowledge, have you ever looked down on someone who cannot do deep theological reasoning, considering their faith deficient? This lesson shows that for the incapable person, taqlid is not sinful. Is there arrogance in your heart? Repent and find a way to teach others without belittling them.
?
The fourth opinion restricts valid taqlid only to sources that yield certainty (Qur'an and mutawatir sunna). Think about where you get your religious information — from social media, popular lectures, or unverified sources? Are you at risk of following something uncertain? Will you check your sources today and ensure they align with what is absolutely established?

The True Scope of the Disagreement on Taqlid
The author clarifies the true scope of the disagreement on taqlid, arguing it is general and applies only to certain muqallids.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the scholarly disagreement about taqlid applies broadly to all types of reasoned inquiry and to all muqallids, and is limited to those who hold their beliefs with certainty.

Core Idea
The disagreement on taqlid is universal in scope but excludes doubt and conjecture.

The author now turns to clarify common misunderstandings about the disagreement on taqlid. He insists that the disagreement is not limited to certain beliefs or certain types of people, but is general. However, he also introduces an important qualification: the disagreement only concerns those who have certainty in their taqlid, not those who doubt or conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very direct style, stating his position and then refuting the alternative.
2
Identify the two aspects of the author's correction
The author corrects two misconceptions: (a) about the scope of nazar (knowledge of God vs other matters) and (b) about the type of muqallid (city vs mountain). Note how he uses citations to support his view.
The author argues that the disagreement is general, not restricted as the commentator claimed.
3
Understand the limitation to the muqallid with certainty
The author restricts the disagreement to the muqallid who is jāzim (certain). Doubters and conjecturers are agreed to have invalid faith.
This is a key condition that many may overlook.
4
Consider the distinction between afterlife and worldly rulings
The author introduces a distinction that affects how we view the sufficiency of taqlid, though the text is incomplete here.
The passage cuts off, but the distinction is important for understanding the practical implications.

And we wish to establish for you here that the correct view is that this disagreement applies to reasoned inquiry leading to knowledge of God Most High, and to other than it, such as reasoned inquiry leading to knowledge of the messengers. Similarly, the correct view is that this disagreement applies to all muqallids, whether they are from the people of cities and villages or whether they were raised on high mountain peaks. From this you learn that the statement of the commentator that follows—after relating the opinions in this issue—'the locus of disagreement is other than reasoned inquiry leading to knowledge of God Most High; as for it, it is obligatory by consensus. Similarly, the disagreement is only concerning one who was raised on a high mountain peak, for example'—is not in accordance with the most correct view in both respects. And to what we have chosen, al-‘Allama al-Amir referred by his statement concerning the first aspect: 'He—i.e., the commentator—followed the Shaykh al-Islam, but Ibn Qasim refuted him by stating that the disagreement is general.' End. And concerning the second aspect: 'The origin of this statement is from al-Sa‘d according to what is known, and the truth—as al-Qadi al-Saktani and al-Yusi said—is the existence of the muqallid, indeed even worse than him among the common people of cities.' End. And he said: This disagreement only occurs among them concerning the muqallid who has certainty (jāzim); as for the doubter and the conjecturer, there is agreement on the invalidity of his faith. Commentary
This passage is a series of clarifications and refinements on the nature of the scholarly disagreement about taqlid. The author first states his position that the disagreement applies broadly to reasoned inquiry (nazar) leading to knowledge of God and also to other objects of belief, such as knowledge of the prophets. He also insists the disagreement covers all muqallids, regardless of whether they live in cities, villages, or remote mountain areas. He then criticizes the commentator (al-Sharh) who had restricted the disagreement to matters other than knowledge of God (which is obligatory by consensus) and only to those raised in remote places like mountain peaks. The author cites al-‘Allama al-Amir to support that the disagreement is general, not restricted, and the true view is that taqlid exists even among urban commoners, as per al-Saktani and al-Yusi. Finally, the author introduces two further limitations: first, the disagreement only applies to the muqallid who holds his belief with certainty (jāzim), not to the doubter or conjecturer, whose faith is unanimously invalid. Second, the dispute about sufficiency of taqlid concerns afterlife rulings; in worldly matters, the criterion for judging the muqallid's faith is based on something else (the text cuts off).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Name one specific step you will take today to strengthen your certainty—whether through seeking knowledge, reflection, or sincere supplication.
?
The passage distinguishes between afterlife and worldly rulings regarding taqlid: the disagreement on sufficiency concerns what is with God of reward and punishment, not how we are judged in this life. Are you satisfied with a faith that only meets worldly standards—appearing Muslim outwardly—while inwardly you may harbor uncertainties that affect your eternal fate? What would it mean for you to truly own your faith, not as a passive imitation (taqlid) but as a conviction that settles your heart before Allah?
?

What doubts have you been avoiding? Confront them now. The Faith of the Muqallid: Four Opinions
Sanusi enumerates and critiques four opinions on the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major opinions on the validity of faith for a blind follower (muqallid) and the author's nuanced position.

Core Idea
Faith through taqlid can be valid, but reasoning remains ideal for those able.

This passage tackles a classic debate in Islamic theology: can a person who simply follows religious teachings without personal reasoning be considered a true believer? Sanusi presents several scholarly positions, ranging from outright invalidation to full acceptance, and adds his own nuanced view that depends on the individual's capacity and circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the flow. Notice how the author enumerates opinions and interjects his own preferred view.
The passage starts with the third opinion (the author's preferred view).
2
Identify the four opinions
Locate the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth opinions. For each, note the key condition that determines whether the muqallid's faith is valid or not.
The third opinion is the author's own, and he supports it with evidence.
3
Understand the author's final position
The author adds a personal qualification to the sixth opinion, distinguishing between those who might be harmed by philosophical inquiry and those who are safe.
This shows a practical, context-sensitive approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه
هو غير عاص ; لأنه ما ترك شيئًا إلا وهو عاجز عن تحصيله، ولا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها، وهذا هو القول الذي رجّحناه في كلامنا السابق , وهو الذي تَنْصُرُه أَدلَّةُ الشَّرع . القول الرابع: أنه إذا قلد القرآن والسُّنَّة المتواترة التي تفيد القطع ; كان إيمانه صحيحا لاتباعه المقطوع به , وإن قلد غير ذلك لم يصح إيمانه ; لأنه لا يأمن الخطأ. القول الخامس : الاكتفاء بالتقليد , وعدم الحكم بعصيانه مطلقا، سواء أكانت عنده أهليَّة النَّظر والاستدلال أم لم تكن. وصاحب هذا القول اعتبر النظر والاستدلال شرطاً لكمال الإيمان، لا لأصل الإيمان , فمن لم تكن عنده أهليّة النظر فهو غير قادر على تحصيله , ومن كان عنده أهليَّة النَّظر , فإنْ نظر واستدلّ فقد كَمَّل إيمانه , وإن لم ينظر، ولم يستدلّ فقد ترك ما الأولى بمَنْ على مثل حاله . القول السادس: أنَّ إيمان المقلد صحيح، وأنَّه يحرم عليه النظر والاستدلال. ولا شك أصحاب هذا القول يريدون تحريم النظر والاستدلال على طريق الفلسفة التي لا تُؤْمَن عاقبتها , أن يكون قولهم هذا خاصا ويجب بمن يُخشى عليه من الأخذ في هذه السبيل أن تتزلزل عقيدته بما يطرأ عليها من آراء الفلاسفة ونظرياتهم , فإن كان بحيث لا يُخشى عليه شيء من ذلك .. فاعتقادي أنه لا يحرم عليه النظر .
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid (blind follower), and clarification of the disagreement that occurs concerning it. He (the muqallid) is not disobedient, because he did not leave anything except that he is incapable of acquiring it, and Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. This is the opinion which we preferred in our previous speech, and it is that which the evidence of the Law supports. The fourth opinion: that if he follows the Qur’an and the frequently transmitted Sunnah which produces certainty, then his faith is valid because of his following of what is certain, but if he follows other than that, his faith is not valid because he is not safe from error. The fifth opinion: sufficiency of blind following, and not judging him as disobedient absolutely, whether he possesses the capacity for independent reasoning and inference or not. The proponent of this opinion considered independent reasoning and inference to be a condition for the perfection of faith, not for the essence of faith. So whoever does not have the capacity for independent reasoning is unable to acquire it, and whoever does have the capacity for independent reasoning, then if he reflects and infers, he has perfected his faith, and if he does not reflect nor infer, he has left what is more appropriate for someone in his situation. The sixth opinion: that the faith of the muqallid is valid, and that independent reasoning is forbidden for him. There is no doubt that the proponents of this opinion intend to forbid independent reasoning by way of philosophy, whose outcome is not safe. Their statement is specific and must be applied to one who is feared for him, if he engages in this path, that his creed may be shaken by what occurs to him from the opinions of the philosophers and their theories. But if he is such that nothing like that is feared for him, then my belief is that independent reasoning is not forbidden for him.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings on the faith of a muqallid (one who follows religious doctrines without independent reasoning). The author presents several opinions. First, he mentions the third opinion (likely from a previous enumeration) that the muqallid is not sinful because he is incapable of acquiring knowledge. He notes that this is his preferred view supported by textual evidence. Then he lists the fourth opinion: that if the muqallid follows the Qur’an and the mutawatir (mass-transmitted) Sunnah, his faith is valid because he is following certain knowledge; but if he follows other sources, his faith is not valid due to the risk of error. The fifth opinion holds that blind following is sufficient regardless of the person's ability to reason; reasoning is a condition for perfection, not for the essence of faith. The sixth opinion says that the muqallid's faith is valid, but independent reasoning is forbidden for him, especially to protect those weak in faith from harmful philosophical ideas. The author adds his own nuance: if no harm is feared, then reasoning is not forbidden. The author thus navigates between strict prohibition and permission based on circumstances.

Key Terms
مقلد
التقليد
النظر
الاستدلال
القطع
المتواترة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all forms of taqlid are invalid and that a muqallid has no faith.
Correction: The author shows that taqlid of certain sources (Qur'an, mutawatir Sunnah) can produce valid faith, and distinguishes between incapacity and negligence.

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the fifth and sixth opinions regarding the muqallid?
A) Fifth says his faith is invalid, sixth says valid.
B) Fifth permits independent reasoning, sixth forbids it absolutely.
C) Fifth says reasoning is for perfection, sixth says it is forbidden for those who might be harmed.
D) Fifth says muqallid is sinful, sixth says he is not.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith can be valid under certain conditions, but personal reasoning remains commendable for those who can do so safely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you are capable of independent reasoning in matters of faith, yet you rely purely on taqlid, are you content with merely valid faith when perfection is within your reach? What concrete step will you take this week to deepen your reasoned understanding of a core Islamic belief?
?
Do you truly know your own level of understanding? Are you a muqallid by necessity or by laziness? If the latter, how does your negligence weigh on your heart, and what will you change starting today?
?
The sixth opinion warns against philosophy due to fear of shaken beliefs. Do you avoid deep questions out of fear, or do you engage with them safely? What is one question about your creed you have been avoiding, and how can you address it with reliable scholars to gain certainty rather than doubt?

The Transmitted Disagreement on the Reality of Faith
Sanusi transmits a disagreement about the definition of iman and its practical ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transmitted disagreement regarding the definition of iman and the condition that verbal confession must not be contradicted by actions that necessitate disbelief.

Core Idea
Faith is not just words; contradictory actions can nullify it.

In this passage, Sanusi mentions that some scholars of kalam have transmitted a disagreement about the nature of faith. An opinion attributed to al-Ash‘arī and al-Qāḍī holds that faith is merely verbal confession. However, this confession is only accepted in practice if it is not accompanied by actions that necessitate disbelief. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on transmitted disagreement
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the disagreement and the condition.
2
Identify the transmitted opinion
Find the opinion that faith is merely verbal confession and the names of those it is attributed to.
al-Ash‘arī and al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī).
3
Understand the condition
Note that the confession is accepted for rulings unless it is accompanied by something contradictory like idol prostration.
4
Consider the practical implication
Think about why this condition matters for distinguishing believers from disbelievers in this world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضُ القوْمِ المصنِّفين في هذا الفنّ يَحْكِي الخُلْفا أي الخِلافَ أهله عن من المتقدمين والمتأخرين . : فمنهم مَن نقل عن الأشعري والقاضي الإقرار باللسان فقط ـ على ما ستعرفه في مسألة حقيقة الإيمان - فكل من أقر أجريت عليه الأحكام الإسلامية , ولم نحكم عليه بالكفر , إلا إن اقترن إقراره بما ينافيه ويقتضي الكفر كالسجود للصنم، وقد ذكر الشارح هذين الأمرين على ما هو الصواب في كل واحد منهما فلا نطيل بالتفريع فيهما . محي. (۱) قوله (صحة إيمانه يندرج تحت هذا محرم النظر . واعلم أن موضع الخلاف التقليد فيما جهله كفر , كصفات السلوب والمعنوية. أما صفات المعاني ونحوها مما لا يكفر منكرها فلا ; كما أفاده العلامة الملوي . الأمير.
And some of the people, the authors in this art, relate the khulf (disagreement) — i.e., the disagreement — from its people among the early and late scholars. Among them is one who transmitted from al-Ash‘arī and al-Qāḍī that (faith is) verbal confession only — as you will come to know in the issue of the reality of faith. So whoever confesses, the Islamic rulings are applied to him, and we do not judge him as disbelief, unless his confession is accompanied by what contradicts it and necessitates disbelief, such as prostrating to an idol. And the commentator (al-shāriḥ) has mentioned these two matters according to what is correct concerning each of them, so we do not prolong by branching out into them. (Marginal note: May he be shielded.) (Footnote: His saying: 'The validity of his faith falls under this — the one for whom reasoning is forbidden. And know that the place of disagreement is concerning following (taqlīd) in matters which ignorance of constitutes disbelief, such as the attributes of negation (ṣifāt al-sulūb) and the attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyya). As for the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī) and similar matters which the denier of them does not become a disbeliever, then no (disagreement applies); as instructed by the scholar al-Mallawī.' — al-Amir.)
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses a different aspect: the transmission of an opinion that faith is merely verbal confession, attributed to al-Ash‘arī and al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī). The author notes that whoever outwardly confesses with the tongue is treated as a Muslim in this world, unless their confession is contradicted by actions like idol prostration. The commentator (shāriḥ) has already clarified this correctly. The marginal note adds that the disagreement about taqlīd applies to matters where ignorance constitutes disbelief, such as the negative attributes (ṣifāt al-salbiyya) and the attributes that are essential (ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyya). For other attributes like ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī (attributes of meanings), denying them does not lead to disbelief, so the discussion on taqlīd is not relevant. This nuance is from the scholar al-Mallawī and al-Amir.

حقيقة الإيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any verbal confession automatically makes a person a Muslim regardless of actions.
Correction: The source clarifies that if the confession is accompanied by actions that necessitate disbelief, such as prostrating to an idol, the person is not considered a Muslim.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does verbal confession not suffice for being treated as a Muslim?
A) When the person is ignorant.
B) When the confession is not made in front of witnesses.
C) When the confession is accompanied by an action like prostrating to an idol.
D) When the person does not believe in his heart.

Key Takeaway
Faith as verbal confession is accepted for worldly rulings unless contradictory actions betray it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your tongue declares faith, but do any of your actions silently deny it? Think of an area where your behaviour contradicts your confession. Will you confront it today, or will you let that contradiction stand as a silent nullification of your claim?
?
If your life were placed before the scale of this lesson—confession weighed against actions—would your deeds confirm or cancel your words? What one change would tip the balance toward truth?
?
The passage warns that a single act like prostration to an idol can nullify your confession. What modern 'idols'—pride, greed, comfort—are you bowing to that contradict your faith? Will you destroy them before they destroy your claim?

Taqlid and the Rulings on Ignorance of Attributes
Footnote distinguishes between attributes whose denial constitutes disbelief and those which do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which matters ignorance of constitutes disbelief and the role of taqlid in matters of faith.

Core Idea
Not all ignorance of theological matters leads to disbelief; only ignorance of essential and negative attributes does.

In the footnote, the author clarifies the scope of disagreement regarding taqlid. He explains that following (taqlid) in matters where ignorance constitutes disbelief, such as the negative attributes (sifāt al-sulūb) and essential attributes (sifāt al-ma‘nawiyya), is a point of disagreement. However, for other attributes like sifāt al-ma‘ānī, denying them does not lead to disbelief, so the discussion on taqlid does not apply. This nuance is important for understanding the boundaries of faith and disbelief in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote passage
Read the Arabic passage of the footnote and its translation. Note the terminology.
2
Identify the categories of attributes
Find the three types mentioned: sifāt al-sulūb (negative attributes), sifāt al-ma‘nawiyya (essential attributes), and sifāt al-ma‘ānī (attributes of meanings).
3
Understand the ruling on taqlid
Note that taqlid is only a point of disagreement regarding matters where ignorance leads to disbelief, i.e., the first two categories.
4
Differentiate the third category
For sifāt al-ma‘ānī, denial does not lead to disbelief, so the discussion on taqlid is irrelevant.
