Islam and Iman: Distinct Yet Inseparable
Author distinguishes Islam and Iman while affirming their inseparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islam and Iman are distinct in essence and concept yet necessarily linked in Sharia, and that Islam is fundamentally inner acceptance of divine rulings.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman are different in meaning but inseparable in practice.

This passage clarifies a key theological distinction: although we often use 'Islam' and 'Iman' interchangeably, scholars have debated whether they refer to the same reality. Here, the author affirms they are different in essence and concept, yet Sharia binds them together so tightly that no Muslim is without Iman and no believer is without Islam. He then defines Islam not merely as outward actions but as inner acceptance (idh'an) of Allah's commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: ذاتا (essence), مفهوم (concept), تلازم (necessary linkage).
Pay attention to the quoted definition from the matn.
2
Understand the relationship statement
The author states that Islam and Iman are different (مختلفان) in essence and concept. This means they are not synonyms. Yet they are necessarily linked (تلازما) in Sharia. Ask yourself: why must they be linked?
3
Analyze the definition of Islam
The quote defines Islam as 'righteous deeds' but clarifies that it really means acceptance (الإذعان) of commands and prohibitions, whether or not one performs them. This shows that the core of Islam is inner submission, not just outward action.
Contrast this with the common view that Islam is only external.
4
Connect the two ideas
How does the definition support the earlier statement? Because Iman also involves inner conviction, the necessary linkage makes sense: both require inner acceptance. Thus no Muslim can lack Iman, and no believer can lack Islam.
This passage guards against separating faith and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في مفهومي الإسلام والإيمان. مختلفان ذاتا ومفهوما وإن تلازما شرعا، بحيث لا يوجد مسلم ليس بمؤمن ولا مؤمن ليس بمسلم. أشار إلى اختيار هذا المذهب بقوله: (والإسلام اشرحن حقيقته بالعمل الصالح، أعني: امتثال المأمورات واجتناب المنهيات، والمراد الإذعان لتلك الأحكام وعدم ردها، سواء عملها أم لم يعملها).
The difference in the concepts of Islam and Iman. They are different in essence and concept, even if they are necessarily linked in Sharia, such that there does not exist a Muslim who is not a believer nor a believer who is not a Muslim. He indicated the choice of this school by his words: 'And Islam, explain its reality as righteous deeds, I mean: compliance with commands and avoidance of prohibitions, and the intended meaning is acceptance (al-idh'an) of those rulings and not rejecting them, whether one performs them or not.'
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between Islam and Iman. It begins by stating that there is a difference (khilaf) in the concepts of Islam and Iman. The author then presents the view that they are different in essence (dhatan) and concept (mafhuman) but necessarily linked in Sharia (talazama shar'an). This means that while the two terms refer to distinct realities, they are inseparable in Islamic law. The author emphasizes that this linkage is such that no Muslim exists who is not a believer, and no believer exists who is not a Muslim. He then points to the selection of this view by quoting the matn: 'And Islam, explain its reality as righteous deeds, meaning: compliance with commands and avoidance of prohibitions, and the intended meaning is acceptance (al-idh'an) of those rulings and not rejecting them, whether one performs them or not.' This shows that the author defines Islam as internal acceptance (idh'an) of the rulings, which is the basis for outward actions. Thus, Islam is not merely outward actions but inner submission.

مفهوم (concept)
تلازم (necessary linkage)
إذعان (acceptance)
امتثال (compliance)
مأمورات (commands)
منهيات (prohibitions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Islam is only outward actions, while Iman is only inner belief.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Islam itself is rooted in inner acceptance (idh'an) of rulings, making it inseparable from Iman. Thus, a person cannot be a true Muslim without inner conviction, and a believer cannot be without submission.

Checkpoint

B) Different in essence but necessarily linked

D) The same in Sharia but different in language

Key Takeaway
Islam and Iman are distinct in essence and concept, yet Sharia makes them inseparable; Islam is fundamentally inner acceptance of divine commands.

The Identity of Islam and Iman
Presents the majority view that Islam and Iman are identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority Maturidi and Ash'ari investigator view that Islam and Iman are identical in concept and existence, making any difference merely verbal.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman are not two separate things – they are the same reality.

The author now presents an alternative view to the earlier distinction between Islam and Iman. The majority of Maturidi scholars and the investigators among the Ash'aris hold that the two terms are united in meaning and application. This passage outlines their reasoning and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key claim: the unity of the two concepts.
2
Break down the claim
Identify the three parts of the view: 1) Unity of what is intended by both terms in Sharia, 2) Equality in existence (whoever has one has the other), 3) Consequence: the dispute is verbal.
The term 'ma'al' (outcome) is crucial for understanding why it's verbal.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If Islam and Iman are identical, what does that mean for someone who claims to be Muslim but lacks inner faith? The answer is that they cannot have one without the other in Sharia.
This resolves the apparent disagreement by showing it is only about wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب جمهور الماتريدية والمحققون من الأشاعرة إلى اتحاد مفهوميهما، بمعنى وحدة ما يراد منهما في الشرع، وتساويهما بحسب الوجود، على معنى أن كل من اتصف بأحدهما فهو متصف بالآخر شرعا، وعليه فالخلاف لفظي باعتبار المآل.
And the majority of the Maturidis and the investigators from the Ash'aris went to the unity of their two concepts, meaning the unity of what is intended by them in Sharia, and their equality with respect to existence, in the sense that everyone who is described by one of them is described by the other in Sharia, and therefore the dispute is verbal with consideration of the final outcome (al-ma'al).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative view. The author states that the majority of the Maturidis and the investigators (muhaqqiqun) among the Ash'aris hold that the two concepts are united (ittihad mafhumayhima). This means that what is intended by both in Sharia is one and the same, and they are equal in existence. That is, anyone who is described by one is described by the other in Sharia. Therefore, the dispute is merely verbal (lafzi) when considering the final outcome (bi-i'tibar al-ma'al). This means that in terms of the final result, both views agree that a person cannot be Muslim without being a believer, but the difference is only in how the terms are defined.

Key Takeaway
The majority of Maturidis and Ash'ari investigators hold that Islam and Iman are identical in meaning and practice, making any difference merely a verbal dispute about the outcome.

The Example of Righteous Deeds: Uttering the Shahadas
The author gives an example of righteous deeds and clarifies the Islam-Iman distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the utterance of the two shahadas serves as the defining righteous deed for Islam and the distinction between external acceptance (Islam) and internal acceptance (Iman).

Core Idea
Islam is external acceptance through utterance; Iman is inner affirmation.

The author now gives a concrete example of the righteous deeds that constitute Islam: the utterance of the two testimonies of faith. He uses this to clarify a crucial distinction between the outward act of submission (Islam) and the inward belief (Iman). This passage helps prevent the common confusion of equating the two without proper distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author introduces the example and then clarifies the terminology.
Pay attention to the logical flow: example → consequence if misinterpreted → correction.
2
Identify the example and its role
The author states that the utterance of the two shahadas is the example of a righteous deed. Understand why this specific act is chosen as the defining act for Islam.
3
Understand the distinction between external and internal acceptance
The passage distinguishes between external acceptance (al-idh'an al-dhahiri) which is Islam, and internal acceptance (al-idh'an al-batini) which is Iman. Grasp why this distinction is important.
This distinction prevents the erroneous merging of faith and submission.
4
Reflect on the logical argument
The author shows that if acceptance were equated with affirmation, Iman would equal Islam, which contradicts the intended distinction. The correction is that Islam is outward submission.
This logical move clarifies the relationship between deeds and faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال هذا يعني العمل الذي فسر به الإسلام: النطق بالشهادتين في المفهوم، وإلا فالإذعان تصديق؛ فيلزم أن الإيمان هو الإسلام، والفرض خلافه، والحق أن الإسلام هو الإذعان الظاهري، لأن الإذعان الباطني هو الإيمان، والإذعان الظاهري يحصل بالنطق بالشهادتين، وبأن يسأل عن الصلاة مثلا فيقول: واجبة.
An example of this means the deed by which Islam is interpreted: the utterance of the two testimonies (al-shahadatayn) in concept, and otherwise, acceptance (al-idh'an) is affirmation (tasdiq); so it would follow that Iman is Islam, but the assumption is opposite, and the truth is that Islam is external acceptance (al-idh'an al-dhahiri), because internal acceptance (al-idh'an al-batini) is Iman, and external acceptance occurs by the utterance of the two testimonies, and by being asked about prayer, for example, and saying: obligatory.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage provides an example of the righteous deeds that constitute Islam. The author explains that the example (mithal) refers to the deeds by which Islam is interpreted: the utterance of the two shahadas (al-nutq bi al-shahadatayn) in concept (fi al-mafhum). He then argues that otherwise, if one takes al-idh'an (acceptance) as tasdiq (affirmation), then it would follow that Iman is Islam, which contradicts the assumption. The truth is that Islam is external acceptance (al-idh'an al-dhahiri), because inner acceptance (al-idh'an al-batini) is Iman. External acceptance occurs by uttering the two shahadas and by answering when asked about prayer, for example, by saying it is obligatory. Thus, the example clarifies that Islam is the outward manifestation of submission.

نطق بالشهادتين (utterance of the two testimonies)
إذعان ظاهري (external acceptance)
إذعان باطني (internal acceptance)
تصديق (affirmation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that merely uttering the shahada without inner conviction is sufficient for Islam, or that Islam and Iman are identical.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Islam is external acceptance (utterance and outward compliance), while Iman is inner affirmation. They are distinct but related.

Checkpoint

B) Uttering the two shahadas
C) Inner affirmation of faith
D) Both external and internal submission

Key Takeaway
The utterance of the two shahadas is the prime example of a righteous deed that constitutes Islam as external submission, distinct from inner faith (Iman).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Sa'd's Reconciliation of the Two Views
Al-Sa'd's reconciliation of the two views on Islam and Iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sa'd reconciles the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on whether Islam and Iman are synonymous, by showing that the difference depends on how Islam is defined.

Core Idea
The disagreement is about definitions, not doctrine.

In this supplement, the author presents al-Sa'd's elegant reconciliation of the two positions. Instead of choosing sides, al-Sa'd shows that the apparent contradiction arises from a difference in how the term 'Islam' is understood. When Islam is taken as external compliance, it differs from Iman; when taken as internal submission, it is identical. This transforms a substantive dispute into a verbal one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a reconciliation that hinges on two definitions.
The passage begins with 'تتمة' indicating it is a supplement.
2
Identify the two interpretations of Islam
Al-Sa'd presents two ways to define Islam: external compliance and internal submission. Find the descriptions for each and notice the contrasting outcomes.
External compliance is outward acts; internal submission includes heart acceptance.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The core idea is that the dispute is not over the reality but over the meaning of the word 'Islam'. Depending on which meaning is adopted, Islam is either different from or identical to Iman.
This makes the conflict verbal, not real.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this reconciliation is valuable. It preserves both positions while showing they are not in true conflict.
This is a common method in Islamic scholarship to harmonize apparently conflicting views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة: جمع السعد بين قول الأشاعرة والماتريدية بالترادف وعدمه: بأنهما خلاف في حال، فإن مفهوم الإسلام إن فسر بالانقياد الظاهري بمعنى امتثال الأوامر والنواهي والعمل بمقتضى تلك الأحكام من غير ملاحظة الإذعان والتسليم القلبي؛ كان مخالفا لمفهوم الإيمان. وإن فسر بالاستسلام والانقياد الباطني، بمعنى قبول تلك الأحكام والإذعان لها وترك الإباء والاستكبار عنها... كان متحدا به. وهذا وجه لصيرورة الخلاف لفظيا غير ما ذكره الشارح.
Supplement: Al-Sa'd reconciled between the saying of the Ash'aris and the Maturidis regarding synonymity and its absence: that they are a difference in state, for if the concept of Islam is interpreted as external compliance (al-inqiyad al-dhahiri), meaning compliance with commands and prohibitions and acting according to those rulings without observing acceptance (al-idh'an) and submission of the heart, it would be contrary to the concept of Iman. And if it is interpreted as submission (al-istislam) and internal compliance (al-inqiyad al-batini), meaning acceptance of those rulings and submission to them and leaving aside pride and arrogance... it would be identical to it. And this is an aspect for the dispute becoming verbal in a way different from what the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the reconciliation by al-Sa'd between the two views. The author notes that it is a supplement (tamima). Al-Sa'd reconciles by saying that the two views are actually differences in state (khilaf fi hal). If the concept of Islam is interpreted as external compliance (al-inqiyad al-dhahiri) – meaning compliance with commands and prohibitions and acting according to those rulings without considering inner acceptance and submission of the heart – then it is different from the concept of Iman. If it is interpreted as internal submission (al-istislam wa al-inqiyad al-batini) – meaning acceptance of those rulings, submission to them, and leaving behind pride and arrogance – then it is identical to Iman. This shows that the dispute becomes verbal in a way different from what the commentator (al-sharih) mentioned. Thus, the reconciliation shows that the apparent disagreement depends on how Islam is defined.

Key Terms
تتمة
جمع
انقياد ظاهري
انقياد باطني
استسلام
إباء
استكبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari and Maturidi positions are irreconcilably opposed.
Correction: Al-Sa'd shows that the difference is merely due to how Islam is defined; both positions can be correct depending on the definition.

Checkpoint

B) When Islam is defined as internal submission and acceptance
C) When Iman is defined as outward actions
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The apparent conflict between Ash'aris and Maturidis on Islam and Iman is resolved by recognizing that the term 'Islam' can mean either external compliance or internal submission.

The Synonymy of Islam and Iman: Maturidi and Ash'ari Views
The author states the position of Maturidis and Ash'aris on the synonymy of Islam and Iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi and Ash'ari position that Islam and Iman are synonymous in Sharia, the nature of the dispute as verbal or real, and the reconciliation through defining Islam as inner surrender.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman are synonymous when Islam is defined as inner submission.

This passage addresses a classic theological question: Are Islam and Iman the same thing or distinct? The majority of Maturidis and leading Ash'aris argue they are synonymous in Sharia, but the apparent dispute is resolved by distinguishing between literal expression and intended meaning. The commentary by Al-Shanwani and the reconciliation by Al-Sa'd provide a nuanced view that clarifies how different definitions of Islam lead to different conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the main passage
Focus on the statement that Islam and Iman are united in concept and equal in existence, and note that the dispute is considered verbal when considering the outcome.
The author states the position of the majority of Maturidis and investigating Ash'aris.
2
Understand the footnote
Al-Shanwani explains that at the literal level the dispute is real, but some scholars define both as inner submission, citing Quranic verses.
This shows the alternative view that both terms refer to inner surrender.
3
Examine Al-Sa'd's reconciliation
Al-Sa'd resolves the apparent disagreement by showing that if Islam is interpreted as outward compliance without inner submission, it differs from Iman; but if it includes inner surrender, they are identical.
This demonstrates how defining terms precisely can resolve theological disputes.

(۱) قوله: (باعتبار المآل أي : وأما باعتبار ظاهر العبارة ؛ فالخلاف معنوي حقيقي ، وهو المناسب لتعبير الشارح بالاختيار في الدخول، والتزمه بعض قائلا : معناهما الإذعان الباطني ، بدليل: كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَنَ ﴾ [المجادلة: ۲۲] أَفَمَن شَرَحَ اللهُ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَم [الزمر: ۲۲] وادعاء الحذف، أي: لقبول الإسلام؛ خلافُ الأصل، وعلى هذا فالنطق دليل عليهما والأعمال كمال لهما. تتمة: جمع السعد بين قول الأشاعرة والماتريدية بالترادف وعدمه: بأنهما خلاف في حال ، فإن مفهوم الإسلام إن فسر بالانقياد الظاهري بمعنى امتثال الأوامر والنواهي والعمل بمقتضى تلك الأحكام من غير ملاحظة الإذعان والتسليم القلبي ؛ كان مخالفا لمفهوم الإيمان. وإن فسر بالاستسلام والانقياد الباطني، بمعنى قبول تلك الأحكام والإذعان لها، وترك الإباء والاستكبار عنها .. كان متحدا به. وهذا وجه لصيرورة الخلاف لفظيا غير ما ذكره الشارح. الشنواني
And the majority of the Maturidis and the investigators among the Ashʿaris went to the unity of their two concepts, meaning the unity of what is intended by them in the Sharia, and their equality in terms of existence, meaning that whoever is described by one of them is described by the other according to Sharia. And upon this, the dispute is verbal in consideration of the outcome (al-maʾāl). (Footnote: His saying: 'in consideration of the outcome' means: And as for the literal expression, the dispute is real and meaningful, and it is appropriate to the commentator's expression of choice in entering, and some adhered to it saying: their meaning is inner submission, as evidence: 'He wrote faith in their hearts' [Al-Mujadila:22] and 'Is he whose heart Allah has opened to Islam' [Az-Zumar:22] and claiming ellipsis, i.e., for acceptance of Islam; contrary to the original, and upon this the utterance is evidence for both [i.e., Islam and Iman] and deeds are perfection for both. Supplement: Al-Sa'd reconciled between the Ashʿaris and Maturidis on synonymy and its negation: that it is a dispute in a state, for if the concept of Islam is interpreted as outward compliance meaning obeying commands and prohibitions and acting according to those rulings without considering inner submission and surrender, then it differs from the concept of Iman. And if it is interpreted as surrender and inner submission, meaning acceptance of those rulings and submission to them, and leaving refusal and arrogance towards them, then it is united with it. And this is a way for the dispute to become verbal other than what the commentator mentioned. Al-Shanwani.

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between Islam and Iman. The author begins by stating the position of the majority of Maturidis and the verifying scholars among the Ashʿaris: they hold that Islam and Iman are synonymous in Sharia, meaning they refer to the same thing, and whoever has one has the other. Therefore, the apparent dispute is only verbal when considering the final outcome (al-maʾāl). The commentator Al-Shanwani explains that if one considers the literal expression, the dispute is real, but if one considers the intended meaning, it is verbal. Some argue they both mean inner submission (al-idhʿān al-bāṭinī), citing Qur'anic verses. Then the author adds a supplement (tamah) that reconciles the two positions: if Islam is defined as outward compliance without inner submission, it differs from Iman; but if defined as inner surrender and acceptance, then they are identical. This shows that the dispute can be resolved by clarifying the definition of Islam.

Key Takeaway

Examples of Righteous Deeds: The Five Pillars
The author provides examples of righteous deeds that constitute Islam: Shahada, Hajj, Salah, Sawm, and Zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five pillars of Islam as practical examples of the righteous deed that is Islam, along with their linguistic and legal definitions.

Core Idea
Islam is not just belief but outward submission expressed through five concrete actions.

In this passage, the author gives specific examples of what constitutes the righteous deed (al-‘amal al-ṣāliḥ) that is Islam. He explains that Islam is outward submission, and then defines each of the five pillars: Shahada, Hajj, Salah, Sawm, and Zakat. By studying these definitions, you will see how Islamic practice is rooted in both linguistic meaning and legal conditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand outward submission (idhʿān ẓāhirī)
Read the opening lines that distinguish between inward belief (imān) and outward submission (islām). Notice that islām is defined as outward actions like uttering the shahada and affirming obligations.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding confusion between imān and islām.
2
Study each pillar's definition
Go through each example: Hajj, Salah, Sawm, and Zakat. For each, note the linguistic meaning and the legal definition with its conditions.
Pay attention to unique terms like 'non-existent worship' for Sawm and the conditions for Zakat.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that all these acts must be submitted to and accepted without rejection or arrogance. Consider why this is important for understanding Islam as a complete submission.

(أمثلة على العمل الصالح الذي هو الإسلام] مِثَالُ هَذَا) (۲) يعني العمل الذي العمل الذي فُسِّرَ به الإسلام: النُّطق بالشهادتين في المفهوم، وإلا فالإذعان تصديق ؛ فيلزم أن الإيمان هو الإسلام، والفرض خلافه، والحق أن الإسلام هو الإذعان الظاهري، لأن الإذعان الباطني هو الإيمان، والإذعان الظاهري يحصل بالنطق بالشهادتين ، وبأن يُسأل عن الصلاة مثلا، فيقول: واجبة . الشنواني . (۲) قوله: (مثال هذا هذا من باب تنزيل الجزئيات على الكليات ولذا عبّر بالمثال الذي هو جزئي يُذكر لإيضاح القاعدة، ولا يُشترط صحته بخلاف الشاهد الشنواني . التاسعة ، ، وهو أمثلة على العمل الصالح الذي هو الإسلام المُتقدِّم بيانه ، و(الحَجُّ) (۱) المفروض في الخامسة، وقيل: في غيرها إلى لغة: القصد لِمُعظَّم، وشرعاً: عبادة يلزمها وقوف بعرفة ليلةَ عاشر ذي الحجة ، (وَالصَّلَاةُ) المفروضة قبل الهجرة بسنة ، وهي لغة: الدُّعاء، وأما شرعاً فهي أقوال وأفعال مُفتتحة بالتكبير مختتمة بالتسليم، (كَذَا الصِّيَامُ) المفروض في ثانية الهجرة، وهو لغة: الإمساك، وشرعاً: عبادة عدميَّة (۲) ، وقتها طلوع الفجر حتَّى الغروب، فَادْرِ) أي : اعْلَم (وَالزَّكَاة) المفروضة في ثانية الهجرة ، وقيل في غيرها، وهي لغة: النمو والتطهير وأما شرعاً: فهي إخراج جُزءٍ مِن المال، شرط وجوبه لمستحقه بلوغ المال نصابًا ، وبلوغ غروب (۳) عيد الفطر أو فجره (٤) لواجدٍ له ، فَضَلَ عن قوته وقوت عياله(٥) ، لم يتوجه وجوبه على غيره، والمراد: إذعان المذكورات وتسليمها ، وعدم مقابلتها بالرَّد والاستكبار. (۱) قوله: (الحج) قدّمه للنظم ؛ وإن كانت الصلاة أفضل، خلافا للقاضي، حيث قال إن الحج أفضل ؛ لاشتماله على المال والبدن ، ولأنا دعينا إليه ونحن في أصلاب آبائنا. ولا ينافي أفضلية الصلاة ما قالوه في الفروع من تقديم الوقوف على الصلاة حيث خاف فوته ؛ لمزيد مشقة الحج وعدم إمكانه كل وقت، ودين الله يسر . الشنواني . (۲) قوله : (عدمية) نسبة للعدم بمعنى الترك والكف ، لا العدم المحض لأنه لا تكليف إلا بفعل . الأمير. (۳) في نسخة : (وغروب الشمس ليلة عيد الفطر) (٤) قوله: ( بلوغ غروب عيد الفطر) أي: إدراك آخر جزء من رمضان وأول جزء من ليلة شوال كما قال إمامنا الشافعي وأحمد وابن قاسم من المالكية. وقوله: (أو فجره) أي: بلوغ طلوع فجره كما قال أبو حنيفة وبعض المالكية. الشنواني . (٥) في المطبوع زيادة: وقوت عياله يومَه وليلته فقوله يومه وليلته؛ غير موجودة في جميع النسخ الستة .
Examples of the righteous deed that is Islam: An example of this means the deed with which Islam is interpreted: the utterance of the two testimonies (al-shahādatān) in concept, otherwise submission is belief; so it would imply that Iman is Islam, but the contrary is assumed. The truth is that Islam is outward submission, because inner submission is Iman, and outward submission is attained by uttering the two testimonies, and by being asked about prayer, for example, and saying: obligatory. Al-Shanwani. (Footnote 2:) His saying: 'An example of this' is from the category of applying particulars to universals, therefore he expressed it as an example which is a particular mentioned to clarify the rule, and its correctness is not stipulated unlike the witness. Al-Shanwani. The ninth, and it is examples of the righteous deed that is Islam previously explained, and (Hajj) prescribed in the fifth year, and it is said in other years; linguistically: intending a venerated thing; legally: a worship that requires standing at Arafat on the night of the tenth of Dhu al-Hijjah. And (Salah) prescribed one year before the Hijra; linguistically: supplication; legally: words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim. Likewise (Sawm) prescribed in the second year of the Hijra; linguistically: abstention; legally: a non-existent worship [i.e., consisting of abstention], its time from dawn to sunset. So know (and Zakat) prescribed in the second year of the Hijra, and it is said in other years; linguistically: growth and purification; legally: extracting a portion of wealth, its obligation is conditioned by the wealth reaching the nisab, and reaching the sunset of the night of Eid al-Fitr or its dawn for one who possesses it, and it exceeds his own sustenance and that of his dependents; its obligation does not fall on another. And the intent: submission (idhʿān) to these mentioned acts and surrendering to them, and not opposing them with rejection and arrogance. (Footnotes as in original.)
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage provides the actual examples of the righteous deed that is Islam. The author first gives the example of uttering the two shahadas (the testimony of faith). He explains that Islam is outward submission, which is attained by uttering the shahadas and by affirming the obligation of prayers, etc. Then he moves to other pillars: Hajj (pilgrimage) is defined linguistically and legally, with the condition of standing at Arafat. Salah (prayer) is defined as specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim. Sawm (fasting) is defined as abstention from dawn to sunset, described as a 'non-existent worship' meaning it consists of refraining. Zakat (obligatory charity) is defined as extracting a portion of wealth, with conditions of nisab and time. The author concludes that all these are to be submitted to and accepted without rejection. The footnotes provide additional details: e.g., why Hajj is mentioned first despite prayer being more excellent, the definition of 'non-existent', and the differing opinions on the time for Zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Islam is only internal belief with no outward actions.
Correction: This passage shows Islam is outward submission, consisting of specific actions like prayer, fasting, and charity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of Salah (prayer)?
A) Supplication only.
B) Words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim.
C) Standing at Arafat.
D) Abstention from food and drink.

Key Takeaway
The righteous deed that is Islam consists of specific outward acts of submission, each with defined conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Islam is outward submission as this passage teaches, have you considered whether your own performance of the pillars is truly a submission or merely a habit? Will you examine your salah today to see if it begins with takbir and ends with taslim as the passage defines, or has it become a mechanical act? Commit to checking one of your prayers tomorrow.
?
The passage ends by saying the intent is submission to these acts and not opposing them with rejection and arrogance. Is there any pillar that you have been resisting in your heart, whether in its full implementation or in accepting its conditions? Will you repent tonight from any hidden arrogance towards Allah's commands?
?
The passage distinguishes outward submission from inner belief. Are you content with outward compliance while your heart remains arrogant? Have you ever questioned the conditions of Zakat or Sawm as if they are too burdensome? This passage calls for acceptance without rejection. Will you write down one condition of one pillar that you have overlooked and make a firm intention to fulfill it this week?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Can Faith Increase and Decrease?
Introducing the topic of increase and decrease of faith and stating the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basis for the debate on whether faith increases and decreases, and why it relies on the relationship between deeds and faith.

Core Idea
Whether faith can change depends on whether deeds are part of its perfection.

Having established that righteous deeds have a role in faith in terms of perfection, the author now introduces a natural consequence: if deeds are connected to faith's perfection, then faith can increase with more deeds and decrease with less. This lesson presents the matn and its immediate explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the heading and matn
Start by reading the heading and the poetic matn to get a sense of the topic and the core positions stated.
The matn is a concise statement that captures the two main views.
2
Note the key concept of 'perfection' (kimaliyya)
The commentary explains that deeds have 'madkhaliyya' (entrance) into faith in terms of perfection, not essence. This is crucial: the increase and decrease talk is about faith's perfection, not its reality.
3
Distinguish the two views
The matn presents two positions: (1) faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience, and (2) there is no increase or decrease. Understand that the first is 'preferred' by a group of scholars.
The phrase 'la khulfa kadha nuqila' indicates that the denial of change is also transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبول الإيمان الزيادة والنقصان وبيان الخلاف فيه  
يُربِّحَتْ زِيَادَةُ الإِيمَانِ بِمَا تَزِيدُ طَاعَةُ الإِنْسَانِ وَقِيلَ لا خُلْفَ كَذَا قَدْ نُقِلا ونَقْصُهُ بِنَقْصِهَا وَقِيلَ لا  

ولمَّا ذَكرَ أنَّ للأعمال الصَّالحةِ مَدْخليَّةٌ فى الإيمان بالكمالية عندنا ، ذكر هنا أنَّه يتفرّع على تلك المدخليّة القول بزيادة الإيمان ونقصه، فقال: (وَرُبِّحَتْ زِيَادَةُ الإِيمَانِ) أي : ورَبَّحَ جماعةٌ من العلماء القول بقبول زِيَادَةُ الإِيمَانِ بِمَا تَزِيدُ طَاعَةُ الإِنْسَانِ وَقِيلَ لا خُلْفَ كَذَا قَدْ نُقِلا ونَقْصُهُ بِنَقْصِهَا وَقِيلَ لا
Acceptance of faith - its increase and decrease and clarification of the disagreement therein.  It is preferred [to say] the increase of faith by what increases the obedience of a person, and it is said: no [increase] - there is no disagreement, thus it has been transmitted - and its decrease by its decrease, and it is said: no.  And when he mentioned that righteous deeds have an entrance [into] faith in terms of perfection according to us, he mentioned here that it branches from that entrance the statement of increase and decrease of faith, and he said: 'And it is preferred [to say] the increase of faith' i.e., a group of scholars preferred the statement of accepting the increase of faith by what increases the obedience of a person, and it is said: no - there is no disagreement, thus it has been transmitted - and its decrease by its decrease, and it is said: no.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage opens a discussion on whether faith increases and decreases. It begins with a heading indicating the topic. Then a poetic line (matn) states the core: some hold that faith increases with increased obedience and decreases with disobedience, while others deny change. The commentator explains that previous discussion established that righteous deeds have a role in faith in terms of perfection (not essence), and now it follows that faith can increase or decrease accordingly. The phrase 'it is preferred to say the increase of faith' refers to a group of scholars who favor this view. The matn also records the opposing view that there is no increase. This lesson only includes the introductory explanation; the detailed positions are in a footnote not included here.

طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the discussion is about increase and decrease in faith's essence, not its perfection.
Correction: The passage specifies that deeds have 'madkhaliyya' in faith in terms of perfection (kimaliyya), not in its reality. Thus the increase and decrease concern the perfection of faith, not its essential nature.

Checkpoint
According to the matn, how is the increase of faith associated?
A) With increase in knowledge
B) With increase in obedience of a person
C) With increase in wealth
D) With increase in lifespan

Key Takeaway
Faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience according to one view, while another view denies any change; the discussion is about faith's perfection, not its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience, examine your life this past week: which specific acts of obedience did you perform that actually felt like they strengthened your faith? Which acts of disobedience left you feeling spiritually weaker?
?
If faith's increase depends on your actions, are you living as if your faith is growing or shrinking? What one change in your daily routine could you make today to actively seek an increase in your faith?
?
The lesson says the discussion is about the perfection of faith, not its essence. Does your perception of your faith focus more on its presence or its quality? When you say 'my faith is weak,' do you mean it is less perfect, or do you suspect it might be absent? How can you remind yourself that weakness is a call to action, not despair?
